Attribution of Mā'iyyah to Abū Ḥanīfa: Analysis
Al-Nasafī evaluates the report of mā'iyyah attributed to Abū Ḥanīfa, defending against anthropomorphism and questioning its authenticity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the attribution of mā'iyyah to Abū Ḥanīfa is considered weak and how it can be interpreted without implying anthropomorphism.

Core Idea
Mā'iyyah does not imply homogeneity; its attribution to Abū Ḥanīfa is historically suspect.

In this passage, Imām al-Nasafī addresses a controversial report that Abū Ḥanīfa affirmed mā'iyyah (what-ness) for God. He first clarifies that affirming mā'iyyah does not necessitate belief in God's similarity to creation. He then scrutinizes the transmission of this view, questioning its reliability and suggesting alternative interpretations.

Guided Path
1
Understand al-Maturidi's clarification
Read the opening where the author cites al-Maturidi's answer to show that mā'iyyah does not entail homogeneity. Note that this sets the theological framework.
Al-Maturidi's response is key to showing the compatibility of mā'iyyah with tanzih.
2
Examine the transmission of the report
The author notes that the explicit interpretation of mā'iyyah is not from Abu Hanifa but from Dirar, and it is ambiguous.
This raises doubts about the authenticity of the attribution.
3
Consider the possible interpretation
The author suggests that mā'iyyah might refer to the unknown divine name, aligning with a prophetic supplication.
This alternative removes any hint of anthropomorphism.
4
Review Hammad ibn Abi Hanifa's statement
Hammad's balanced position shows the family's stance against both anthropomorphism and negation.
This further distances Abu Hanifa from a literal understanding of mā'iyyah.
5
Identify the weak chain via Ghassan
The author explains that the attribution ultimately comes from Ghassan, whose narrations are unreliable.
This historical criticism renders the report weak.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من شيء بل هو يكون الأشياء كذا ذكره الشيخ أبو منصور في جواب السؤال: إن الله تعالى ما هو ؟ وعرف بهذا أن من أثبت المائية لم يثبت المجانسة بينه وبين المخلوقات، ولا المماثلة، والله الموفق. ثم نقول: إن من روى عن أبي حنيفة رحمه الله القول بالمائية لم يرو عنه التفسير نصا . وذكر أبو محمد النوبختي نصا أن تفسيره لم يرد إلا عن ضرار، وقد بينا أنها محتملة ومن الجائز أنه أراد ذلك الاسم أي الله تعالى اسم لا يعلمه إلا هو، ولو كان مراده هذا فهو موافق لما ورد به الخبر، وهو الدعاء المأثور عن النبي عليه السلام فيمن أصابه هم: اللهم أنا عبدك وابن عبدك وابن أمتك ناصيتي بيدك ماض في حكمك عدل في قضاؤك، أسألك بكل اسم هو لك سميت به نفسك أو أنزلته في كتابك أو علمته أحداً من خلقك أو استأثرت به في علم الغيب عندك أن تجعل القرآن ربيع قلبي ونور صدري وجلاء حزني وذهاب همي. وإذا كان هذا محتملا لا معنى لصرف ذلك إلى إثبات المجانسة والمماثلة مع ظهوره في التبرئ عن التشبيه، وثبتت الرواية عن حماد بن أبي حنيفة، وكان متكلما عالما بمذاهب أبيه أنه قال: لا نفر عن الصفة فرار جهم، ولا نصفه صفة مقاتل بن سليمان، وإنما قال ذلك لأن مقاتلا كان مشبها، وجهم كان معطلا، فتبرأ منهما على أن هذا كله تكلف لأن القول بالمائية غير ثابت عنه على ما مر، وإنما أخذ المتكلمون نسبة القول بالمائية إليه عن غسان فإنه قال: كان أبو حنيفة وأصحابه يقولون بالمائية وخالفهم بشر بن غياث في ذلك، واختار غسان هذا القول، وذكر مناظرة له مع بشر بن غياث إلا أن غياثا لا يقبل منه هذه الرواية لأنه روي عنه سوى هذا أشياء اشتهر مذهب أبي حنيفة في ذلك بخلافها، والله الموفق. وللقائلين بالمائية اختلاف فيما بينهم، تفرد واحد منهم بتقرير مذهبه، ولكل فريق متعلق من الشبه وللمتكلمين عليهم ردود، وقد أعرضنا عن ذلك كله، لأن كتابنا هذا يضيق عن الكلام في مثل تلك المسائل لما مر أن غرضنا من هذا إيراد ما جل من الدلائل فيما اشتهر من المسائل.
From something? Rather, He is the originator of things. Thus mentioned al-Shaykh Abu Mansur in answer to the question: 'What is Allah, exalted is He?' And by this it is known that whoever affirms mā'iyyah (the 'what-ness') has not affirmed homogeneity between Him and created beings, nor likeness. And Allah is the grantor of success. Then we say: Indeed, whoever narrated from Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, the statement of mā'iyyah did not narrate from him an explicit interpretation. And Abu Muhammad al-Nawbakhti mentioned explicitly that his interpretation has not come except from Dirar. And we have shown that it is susceptible to multiple interpretations, and it is possible that he intended that Name, i.e., Allah, exalted is He, is a name that no one knows but He. And if this was his intent, then it agrees with what has come in the report, namely the supplication reported from the Prophet, peace be upon him, concerning one afflicted by distress: 'O Allah, I am Your servant, son of Your servant, son of Your maidservant; my forelock is in Your hand; Your judgment is executed upon me; Your decree is just concerning me. I ask You by every name that is Yours, by which You have named Yourself, or revealed in Your Book, or taught anyone of Your creation, or kept solely to Yourself in the knowledge of the unseen with You, to make the Qur'an the spring of my heart, the light of my chest, the remover of my sorrow and the dispeller of my worry.' And since this is possible, there is no sense in diverting it to affirming homogeneity and likeness, especially with its clear import of disavowing anthropomorphism. And the narration from Hammad ibn Abi Hanifa is established, and he was a theologian, knowledgeable about his father's doctrines, that he said: 'We do not flee from the attribute like the fleeing of Jahm, nor do we describe Him with the description of Muqatil ibn Sulayman.' He only said that because Muqatil was an anthropomorphist and Jahm was a negator, so he disavowed both. However all of this is forced, because the statement of mā'iyyah is not established from him, as has preceded. Rather, the theologians took the attribution of the statement of mā'iyyah to him from Ghassan, for he said: 'Abu Hanifa and his companions used to say mā'iyyah, and Bishr ibn Ghiyath opposed them in that, and Ghassan chose this statement, and mentioned a debate between him and Bishr ibn Ghiyath, except that Ghiyath cannot be trusted regarding this narration because besides this there are things narrated from him that contradict the well-known doctrine of Abu Hanifa in that matter.' And Allah is the grantor of success. And those who affirm mā'iyyah differ among themselves; each one of them singularly reports his doctrine, and each group has some specious argument, and the theologians have refutations against them. And we have turned away from all of that, because our book here is too narrow for discussion of such issues, since our purpose in this is to present the major proofs concerning well-known issues.
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage is a discussion and evaluation of the report that Abu Hanifa held the doctrine of mā'iyyah (affirming 'what-ness' for God). The author begins by citing Shaykh Abu Mansur al-Maturidi's answer to the question 'What is Allah?' to show that affirming mā'iyyah does not entail homogeneity or likeness between God and creation. Then the author addresses the claim that Abu Hanifa taught mā'iyyah. He first notes that those who transmitted this view did not provide an explicit interpretation from Abu Hanifa. Abu Muhammad al-Nawbakhti stated that the interpretation only comes from Dirar, not from Abu Hanifa. The author suggests that the term mā'iyyah could be interpreted as referring to the divine Name that only God knows, which would align with a well-known prophetic supplication. Since this interpretation is possible, there is no justification for using the report to claim that Abu Hanifa believed in anthropomorphism. The author then cites a narration from Hammad ibn Abi Hanifa, a theologian knowledgeable in his father's doctrines, who said that he neither flees from attributes like Jahm nor describes God with the attributes of Muqatil, indicating a balanced position. The author concludes that all this discussion is forced because the attribution of mā'iyyah to Abu Hanifa is not firmly established; it was taken from Ghassan, whose reliability is questionable because other narrations from him contradict the well-known position of Abu Hanifa. Finally, the author mentions that those who affirm mā'iyyah hold differing views, each with their own specious arguments, but he chooses not to discuss them in detail because the book's scope is limited to major proofs on well-known issues.

Key Terms

المجانسة
المماثلة
التشبيه
التفسير
الاسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mā'iyyah always implies that God is similar to creation.
Correction: Al-Nasafī shows that affirming mā'iyyah does not entail homogeneity, and it can be interpreted in a way that avoids anthropomorphism.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is the attribution of mā'iyyah to Abu Hanifa not reliable?
A) Because Abu Hanifa explicitly denied it in his writings.
B) Because the only chain of transmission comes from Ghassan, whose narrations contradict Abu Hanifa's well-known position.
C) Because the Qur'an forbids any discussion of divine attributes.
D) Because all early Muslims unanimously rejected mā'iyyah.

Key Takeaway
The attribution of mā'iyyah to Abū Ḥanīfa is historically unreliable and theologically does not necessarily imply anthropomorphism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a theological opinion attributed to a great scholar, do you accept it uncritically, or do you investigate its chain and context as al-Nasafī does here? What steps can you take to emulate this critical attitude in your own search for religious knowledge?
?
The debate over mā'iyyah shows how easily misunderstanding can arise. Are there areas of your belief about Allah where you may have inadvertently leaned toward either tashbih (likening Him to creation) or ta'til (negating His attributes)? How will you seek balance?
?
The possibility that mā'iyyah refers to the unknown Name of Allah is a profound reminder of His transcendence. When you face distress, do you turn to Allah with the comprehensive supplication mentioned in the lesson, recognizing that He alone knows His full reality? Will you commit to reciting this supplication regularly?

Scriptural and Consensus Evidence for a Direction of Allah
Lists scriptural proofs and consensus for Allah being in a direction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scriptural verses and communal practices that those who affirm a direction for Allah rely upon.

Core Idea
Opponents use clear Quranic verses and universal Muslim practice to support direction.

The first part of the opponent's case is based on revealed texts and observed behavior. Before engaging with their rational arguments, we must see what scriptural and traditional proofs they put forward. This lesson presents the verses on Allah's establishment on the Throne, His being in heaven, above servants, and the sending down of revelation, along with the consensus of raising hands in supplication.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the list of verses and the consensus claim.
2
Identify the scriptural proofs
There are six Quranic verses cited. Find each one and note how they suggest direction: istiwā', being in heaven, above, and sending down.
The word 'sending down' (inzāl) is interpreted as spatial descent.
3
Consider the consensus and innate disposition
The passage claims that Muslims universally raise hands upward in supplication, and humans are naturally disposed to believe Allah is above. Reflect on why these are considered proofs.
4
Conclude the lesson
Understand that the opponents use these as primary evidence for direction. The rational arguments come next.
This lesson covers only the scriptural and traditional part.

أما القائلون بأنه تعالى في مكان مخصوص فإنهم يحتجون بالنصوص من نحو قوله تعالى: الرَّحْمَنُ عَلَى الْعَرْشِ اسْتَوَى، وقوله: ثُمَّ اسْتَوَى عَلَى الْعَرْشِ، وقوله: أَمِنْتُمْ مَنْ فِي السَّمَاءِ، وقوله: وَهُوَ الَّذِي فِي السَّمَاءِ إِلَهُ وَفِي الْأَرْضِ إِلَهٌ، وقوله: وَهُوَ الْقَاهِرُ فَوْقَ عِبَادِهِ، وقوله: إِنَّا أَنزَلْنَاهُ فِي لَيْلَةِ الْقَدْرِ والإنزال هو الإرسال من الأعلى إلى الأسفل، وأجمع المسلمين عليه فإنهم عند سؤالهم الحاجات، واشتغالهم بالدعاء والمناجاة يرمون بأبصارهم، ويرفعون أيديهم إلى جهة العلو، وكذا الناس مجبولون على هذا، فإنهم لو تركوا وما هم عليه من الجبلة ولم ينقلوا عنه بالحيل لما اعتقدوا إلا ذلك أعني أنه تعالى بذاته في الجهة العلوية دون غيرها من الجهات، ويتعلقون أيضا بشبه لهم معقولة أحدها: أن الله تعالى موجود قائم بنفسه، والعالم موجود قائم بنفسه، ولن يعقل القائمان بأنفسهما إلا وأحدهما في جهة من صاحبه. والثانية: أنه تعالى لما خلق العالم إما أن خلقه في ذاته، وإما إن خلقه خارج ذاته، فإن خلقه في ذاته فهو محيط بالعالم من جميع جهاته، وإن خلقه خارج ذاته بقي من العالم بجهة من الجهات لا محالة. والثالثة أن الموجودين لا يعقلان موجودين إلا وأحدهما في جهة من الجهات الست من صاحبه أو بحيث هو، فإن الجوهرين كل واحد منهما في جهة من صاحبه فأعراض كل جوهر بحيث هو، والباري موجود، وكذا العالم، وليس الباري جل وعلا بحيث العالم فثبت أنه بجهة من العالم، وربما يقررون هذا من وجه آخر فيقولون: لا نفى للمذكور أشد تحقيقا من نفيه من الجهات الست فمن نفى الباري جل وعلا من الجهات كلها فقد أخبر بعدمه. وهذه المعقولات يتعلق بها من يثبت الجهة دون المكان. ويقولون : يثبت بهذه المعقولات جهة مطلقة ثم يتعين جهة من الجهات الست وهي جهة العلو بالنصوص التي مر ذكرها ولأن جهة فوق جهة مدح، وجهة تحت جهة ذم، والله تعالى ممدوح محمود، منزه عما يوجب الذم والنقيصة.
As for those who say that He, exalted, is in a specific place, they adduce texts such as His saying: 'The Most Merciful established Himself upon the Throne', and His saying: 'Then He established Himself upon the Throne', and His saying: 'Do you feel secure from Him who is in heaven', and His saying: 'And He is the one who is in heaven as God and on earth as God', and His saying: 'And He is the Subduer above His servants', and His saying: 'Indeed, We sent it down on the Night of Decree' – and sending down is sending from above to below. And the consensus of Muslims is upon it, for when they ask for their needs and occupy themselves with supplication and intimate conversation, they raise their eyes and lift their hands towards the direction of above. Likewise, people are innately disposed to this, for if they were left to their innate disposition and not diverted by devices, they would not believe except that – i.e., that He, exalted, is by His essence in the direction of above to the exclusion of other directions. And they also adhere to rational arguments they have: first, that God exists, self-subsistent, and the world exists, self-subsistent, and two self-subsistent beings are not conceivable except one being in a direction from the other. Second: that when He created the world, either He created it within His essence, or He created it outside His essence. If He created it within His essence, then He encompasses the world from all its directions. If He created it outside His essence, then He remains with respect to the world in one of the directions inevitably. Third: that two existents are not conceivable as existent except one of them being in one of the six directions from the other or in its where, for two substances each one of them is in a direction from the other, and the accidents of each substance are in its where. And the Creator exists, and likewise the world. And the Creator, mighty and majestic, is not in the where of the world, so it is established that He is in a direction from the world. And perhaps they formulate this from another perspective, saying: there is no more certain negation of the mentioned than negating Him from the six directions, so whoever negates the Creator, mighty and majestic, from all directions has announced His non-existence. And these rational arguments are adhered to by those who affirm direction without place. They say: through these rational arguments, an absolute direction is established, then a specific direction from the six directions is determined, which is the direction of above, by the texts that have been mentioned, and because the direction of above is praise, and the direction of below is blame, and God, exalted, is praised, lauded, transcendent above that which entails blame and deficiency.
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This lesson presents the case of those who claim that Allah is in a specific direction. The author first lists their scriptural proofs: verses that mention establishing on the Throne, being in heaven, above servants, and sending down. Then he cites consensus on raising hands in supplication and innate disposition. Next, he presents three rational arguments: (1) two self-subsistent beings must be in relation to each other in direction; (2) creation must be either inside or outside the Creator's essence, leading to direction; (3) two existents always have one in one of the six directions from the other, applied to the Creator and world. Then an additional argument: negating all directions implies non-existence. Finally, they conclude that an absolute direction is established and specified as above by texts and moral considerations (above is praise, below is blame). The author presents these without refutation within this lesson, setting up the response.

Key Terms
جهة
مكان
استواء
إنزال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which practice of Muslims do opponents use as evidence for a direction of Allah?
A) Facing the Qibla during prayer
B) Raising hands and eyes upward during supplication
C) Going on pilgrimage to Mecca
D) Reciting the Qur'an aloud

Key Takeaway
The opponents rely on clear Quranic texts, universal Muslim practice, and innate human disposition to argue that Allah is in the direction of above.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you raise your hands in du'a, do you truly feel that Allah is above you, or is it just a habit? What would it take for that act to become a deep conviction?
?
The passage says people are innately disposed to believe Allah is above unless diverted. Where in your life have doubts or 'devices' clouded this natural belief? How will you actively realign your heart?
?
If you were forced to defend that Allah has a direction, which piece of evidence from this lesson would you cling to? Does your personal practice of raising hands in supplication actually reflect your belief, or is it empty ritual?

Rational Arguments for a Direction of Allah
Presents three rational arguments and a final argument for direction of Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three rational arguments used to support the claim that Allah is in a direction, along with the additional argument from negation.

Core Idea
Opponents argue that two self-subsistent beings cannot exist without one being in a direction from the other.

After presenting scriptural evidence, the opponents of the Ash'ari and Maturidi positions offer rational arguments. These are based on metaphysical principles about existence, creation, and the nature of substances. This lesson presents three such arguments and a further argument that denying all directions implies non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the rational arguments passage
Read the Arabic passage and translation. The passage contains three formal arguments (aḥada-hā... thāniya... thālitha) and an additional formulation (min wajh ākhar).
2
Break down each argument
For each of the three arguments, identify the premises and the conclusion. The first is about self-subsistence, the second about creation, and the third about two existents in general.
3
Understand the additional argument
The additional argument says that negating all six directions is the most certain way to negate existence, so denying direction implies denying existence itself.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
See how they specify the direction
The opponents conclude an absolute direction, then use the scriptural texts and moral considerations to specify it as the direction of above.
5
Reflect on the overall strategy
Consider why the opponents feel the need for rational arguments in addition to scripture. This shows they engage with philosophical reasoning.

Refuting the Karrāmiyya on Accidents in Allah's Essence
Refutation of Karrāmiyya on accidents in divine essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Karrāmiyya claim that Allah's essence can be a locus for temporal accidents is rationally impossible, as it leads to an absurd contradiction.

Core Idea
Allah's essence cannot be a locus for temporal events without absurd consequences.

The Karrāmiyya sect held an anthropomorphic view that the divine essence can undergo temporal changes. This lesson examines why this view leads to an impossible contradiction: either making the temporal eternal or the eternal temporal. The author refutes their position with a concise logical argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic Passage
Read the Arabic text once to get a feel for the author's tone and structure. Notice the exaltation phrase and the logical conclusion.
The passage is short but packed with theological weight.
2
Identify the Karrāmiyya Position
The Karrāmiyya claim that the eternal essence of Allah can be a location (maḥall) for temporal accidents (ḥawādith). Understand what this entails: change and temporality in Allah.
3
Grasp the Logical Impossibility
The author states that this claim leads to one of two impossible outcomes: either the temporal becomes eternal (qidam al-muḥdath) or the eternal becomes temporal (ḥudūth al-qadīm). Both are absurd. Reflect on why these are contradictory to the nature of Allah.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والكرامية وإن كانوا لا يبالوا من القول بأن ذات القديم محل الحواث – تعالى الله عما يقول الظالمون علوا كبيرا - ولكن بينا أنه يؤدي إلى إثبات قدم المحدث أو حدوث القديم، وكلاهما محال . والله الموفق.
And the Karrāmiyya – even though they do not care about the statement that the essence of the Eternal is a locus for temporal accidents – exalted is Allah above what the wrongdoers say, a great exaltation – but we have shown that it leads to the establishment of the eternality of the temporal or the temporalization of the Eternal, and both are impossible. And Allah is the giver of success.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage is a direct refutation of the Karrāmiyya sect (a group known for anthropomorphism). The author notes that the Karrāmiyya are unabashed in claiming that the eternal essence (dhāt al-qadīm) can be a locus (maḥall) for temporal accidents (ḥawādith). After exclaiming that Allah is exalted far above what the wrongdoers say (ta‘ālā Allāhu ‘ammā yaqūlu al-ẓālimūn ‘uluwwan kabīran), the author asserts that this doctrine has already been shown to lead to one of two impossible outcomes: either the temporal becomes eternal (qidam al-muḥdath) or the eternal becomes temporal (ḥudūth al-qadīm). Both are absurd. The author concludes with 'And Allah is the giver of success' (wa-Allāhu al-muwaffiq), indicating that the argument is decisive and relies on divine guidance. This brief paragraph summarizes the conclusion of the longer rational argument presented earlier, applying it specifically to the Karrāmiyya position.

Key Terms
كرامية
ذات القديم
محل الحوادث
قدم المحدث
حدوث القديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the Karrāmiyya position is acceptable because it upholds Allah's power to do anything, including being a locus for accidents.
Correction: The author shows that this belief directly contradicts the nature of the Eternal, leading to logical impossibilities that cannot be reconciled with sound reason and the attributes of Allah.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main criticism of the Karrāmiyya claim that the divine essence is a locus for temporal accidents?
A) It contradicts the consensus of the Muslims.
B) It leads to either making the temporal eternal or the eternal temporal.
C) It implies Allah is composed of parts.
D) It denies the attribute of power.

Key Takeaway
The Karrāmiyya position on divine accidents is logically impossible because it entails either making the temporal eternal or the eternal temporal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that a claim about Allah can be refuted by logical necessity. Is there any belief you hold about God that you have never subjected to rational scrutiny? What would it take for you to examine it honestly?
?
The Karrāmiyya were careless with their words about Allah ('they do not care'). Have you ever spoken about Allah without considering the implications of your words? How can you train yourself to be more precise and reverent in your speech about Him?
?
The proof here relies on two simple premises leading to a contradiction. Do you find yourself relying more on emotion or tradition when it comes to matters of ʿaqīda? Make a commitment to learn the rational arguments that defend tawḥīd so that your faith is built on certainty.

Sects and Disagreements
beginner

Refutation of the Karramiyyah: God is not a locus for events
Nasafi refutes the Karramiyyah by showing their claim entails impossible consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Karramiyyah's claim that God is a locus for originated events is rationally impossible.

Core Idea
God cannot be a locus for events; that would make Him originated.

Imam Nasafi begins by addressing a deviant sect who dared to say that the Eternal Essence is a place where temporal events occur. He shows that this leads to a logical contradiction: either the temporal becomes eternal or the Eternal becomes temporal. This passage establishes the rational impossibility of such a claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the claim of the Karramiyyah and Nasafi's response.
Notice the phrase 'لا يبالوا' indicating their audacity.
2
Identify the two outcomes
Find the two impossible consequences Nasafi mentions: the eternity of the originated or the origination of the Eternal.
Both are logically absurd.
3
Understand the reasoning
Reflect on why each outcome is impossible: a necessary being cannot receive accidents, and an accident cannot become eternal.
This principle is foundational in kalam.
4
Connect to transcendence
Realize that this refutation upholds God's transcendence above change and temporality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والكرامية وإن كانوا لا يبالوا من القول بأن ذات القديم محل الحوادث – تعالى الله عما يقول الظالمون علوا كبيرا - ولكن بينا أنه يؤدي إلى إثبات قدم المحدث أو حدوث القديم، وكلاهما محال. والله الموفق. ولأن اختصاصه بالعرش إما أن كان لاقتضاء ذاته ذلك، وإما أن كان لاقتضاء معنى، وأما أن كان لا لذاته، ولا لمعنى ولا جائز أن يكون لاقتضاء ذاته لوجود ذاته في الأزل، ولا اختصاص له بالعرش، ولا جائز أن يكون لاقتضاء معنى، لأن المعنى إن كان قديما فلم يكن الاختصاص في القديم ثابتا فانعدام الاختصاص مع قيام المعنى الموجب له محال، وإن كان محدثا فقيام المحدث به محال على ما مر، وإن كان اختصاصه بالعرش لا لاقتضاء ذاته، ولا لاقتضاء معنى جاز أن يختص بجميع أجسام العالم فيتمكن من الصفحة السفلى من العالم، أو من الأرض، أو من الآبار، أو في بطون الحيوانات، وأشباه ذلك، وذلك كله محال، دل أن تمكنه في المكان محال. وهو من أمارات الحدوث، وبالله المعونة.
And the Karramiyyah, even if they do not care about the statement that the essence of the Eternal is a locus for originated things—exalted is Allah above what the wrongdoers say, with a great exaltation—but we have shown that it leads to affirming the eternity of the originated or the origination of the Eternal, both of which are impossible. And Allah is the grantor of success. And because His specification (ikhtiṣāṣ) to the Throne is either due to His essence requiring that, or due to a meaning requiring it, or it is neither due to His essence nor due to a meaning. And it is not possible that it be due to His essence requiring that, because His essence existed in pre‑eternity and He had no specification to the Throne. And it is not possible that it be due to a meaning, because if the meaning is eternal, then the specification should have been established in pre‑eternity—so the absence of specification despite the existence of the meaning that necessitates it is impossible. And if it is originated, then its subsistence in Him is impossible, as has passed. And if His specification to the Throne is not due to His essence requiring it nor due to a meaning, then it would be permissible for Him to be specified to all bodies of the world, so He could occupy the lowest surface of the world, or the earth, or wells, or the insides of animals, and the like—all of which is impossible. This shows that His occupation of place is impossible. And it is one of the signs of origination. And with Allah is the assistance.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage combines a direct refutation of the Karramiyyah (who claim God is a locus for events) with a logical argument about God's supposed specification to the Throne. The refutation: their position leads to either the eternity of the originated (if God receives accidents) or the origination of the Eternal—both impossible. Then the specification argument: If God is specifically on the Throne, this specification must have a cause. It cannot be essential because God existed without it. It cannot be due to a meaning (cause) because that meaning would be either eternal (then specification should have been eternal) or originated (then it cannot subsist in God). If it is uncaused, then He could be specified to any place, leading to absurdities like being in wells. Therefore, spatial location is impossible and is a sign of origination, so it cannot apply to God.

Key Terms
الكرامية
محل الحوادث
القديم
المحدث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some claim that God can be a location for temporal events.
Correction: This would entail either the eternality of the temporal or the temporality of the Eternal, both impossible.

Checkpoint
What does Nasafi say is an impossible consequence of the Karramiyyah's claim?
A) God's essence becomes a locus
B) The originated becomes eternal
C) The Eternal becomes originated
D) Both B and C

Key Takeaway
The claim that God is a locus for events leads to logical absurdities, affirming His transcendence above change.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever imagined that your worship 'moves' God or changes His attitude toward you? This passage declares that God is beyond all change. Will you let go of a transactional view of worship and instead adore a God who is eternally perfect and unaffected by your actions? How will you adjust your relationship with Him knowing that He does not 'respond' to you as creatures do?
?
The Karramiyyah dared to describe God as a 'place' for events. In your heart, do you ever think of God as somewhere in space, or as experiencing time? This lesson shows that such thoughts are logically absurd. Take a moment to repent of any subtle anthropomorphism you may hold. How will you retrain your mind to think of God as absolutely transcendent?
?
If God cannot be a locus for events, then nothing you do—no sin, no good deed—alters His essence. Does that comfort you or unsettle you? Are you willing to worship a God who is entirely independent of your actions, or do you secretly wish you could affect Him? Write down one concrete way you will remind yourself of His transcendence this week.

The argument against God being in a place: specification to the Throne
Nasafi argues that God's specification to any place is impossible by examining the causes of specification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that God cannot be spatially located, especially not on the Throne, because any specification would be either essential or by a cause, both impossible.

Core Idea
God cannot be in a place; location is a sign of createdness.

Having refuted the Karramiyyah, Imam Nasafi now presents a deeper argument against any notion of God occupying a specific place, such as the Throne. He systematically examines the possibilities for why God would be specified to the Throne and shows each leads to an impossibility. This establishes that spatial location is a characteristic of originated beings, not the Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic and translation to see the three possibilities Nasafi enumerates.
Notice the structure: three possibilities each refuted.
2
Examine the first possibility: essential
Understand why essential specification fails: God existed without it in pre-eternity.
3
Examine the second possibility: by a meaning
Understand why a cause fails: if eternal, it should have always been; if originated, it cannot subsist in God.
This relies on the prior refutation of the Karramiyyah.
4
Examine the third possibility: no cause
See the reductio ad absurdum: if no cause, God could be anywhere, even filthy places.
5
Draw the conclusion
Conclude that spatial location is impossible for God and is a sign of origination.
This upholds divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والكرامية وإن كانوا لا يبالوا من القول بأن ذات القديم محل الحوادث – تعالى الله عما يقول الظالمون علوا كبيرا - ولكن بينا أنه يؤدي إلى إثبات قدم المحدث أو حدوث القديم، وكلاهما محال. والله الموفق. ولأن اختصاصه بالعرش إما أن كان لاقتضاء ذاته ذلك، وإما أن كان لاقتضاء معنى، وأما أن كان لا لذاته، ولا لمعنى ولا جائز أن يكون لاقتضاء ذاته لوجود ذاته في الأزل، ولا اختصاص له بالعرش، ولا جائز أن يكون لاقتضاء معنى، لأن المعنى إن كان قديما فلم يكن الاختصاص في القديم ثابتا فانعدام الاختصاص مع قيام المعنى الموجب له محال، وإن كان محدثا فقيام المحدث به محال على ما مر، وإن كان اختصاصه بالعرش لا لاقتضاء ذاته، ولا لاقتضاء معنى جاز أن يختص بجميع أجسام العالم فيتمكن من الصفحة السفلى من العالم، أو من الأرض، أو من الآبار، أو في بطون الحيوانات، وأشباه ذلك، وذلك كله محال، دل أن تمكنه في المكان محال. وهو من أمارات الحدوث، وبالله المعونة.
And the Karramiyyah, even if they do not care about the statement that the essence of the Eternal is a locus for originated things—exalted is Allah above what the wrongdoers say, with a great exaltation—but we have shown that it leads to affirming the eternity of the originated or the origination of the Eternal, both of which are impossible. And Allah is the grantor of success. And because His specification (ikhtiṣāṣ) to the Throne is either due to His essence requiring that, or due to a meaning requiring it, or it is neither due to His essence nor due to a meaning. And it is not possible that it be due to His essence requiring that, because His essence existed in pre‑eternity and He had no specification to the Throne. And it is not possible that it be due to a meaning, because if the meaning is eternal, then the specification should have been established in pre‑eternity—so the absence of specification despite the existence of the meaning that necessitates it is impossible. And if it is originated, then its subsistence in Him is impossible, as has passed. And if His specification to the Throne is not due to His essence requiring it nor due to a meaning, then it would be permissible for Him to be specified to all bodies of the world, so He could occupy the lowest surface of the world, or the earth, or wells, or the insides of animals, and the like—all of which is impossible. This shows that His occupation of place is impossible. And it is one of the signs of origination. And with Allah is the assistance.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage combines a direct refutation of the Karramiyyah (who claim God is a locus for events) with a logical argument about God's supposed specification to the Throne. The refutation: their position leads to either the eternity of the originated (if God receives accidents) or the origination of the Eternal—both impossible. Then the specification argument: If God is specifically on the Throne, this specification must have a cause. It cannot be essential because God existed without it. It cannot be due to a meaning (cause) because that meaning would be either eternal (then specification should have been eternal) or originated (then it cannot subsist in God). If it is uncaused, then He could be specified to any place, leading to absurdities like being in wells. Therefore, spatial location is impossible and is a sign of origination, so it cannot apply to God.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God sits on the Throne like a king.
Correction: This would imply He is subject to place and time, which are created attributes; true transcendence means He is free from spatial confinement.

Checkpoint

A) He is not on the Throne
B) He could be anywhere absurdly
C) He is eternal
D) He is a body

Key Takeaway
Spatial location is a mark of created beings; the Creator is beyond all place.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray, do you ever unconsciously imagine God as being in a certain direction (e.g., above the sky)? How does this argument challenge that imagination, and what does it demand of you instead?
?
If spatial location is a sign of createdness, what does your attachment to specific places (e.g., a special prayer spot, a sacred city) reveal about your understanding of God? Are you treating those places as ends rather than means?
?
The argument shows that God's specification to any place would lead to absurdity. Now consider your own heart: do you confine God to certain moments (e.g., Ramadan, Jumu'ah) rather than living in constant awareness of His transcendence? What would change if you took this truth to heart today?

Refuting the Mu'tazila and Najjariyya: God Everywhere by Knowledge
Nasafi introduces the Mu'tazila/Najjariyya claim to be refuted.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific claim made by the Mu'tazila and Najjariyya that God is everywhere in the sense of knowledge, and why this claim is the target of the upcoming refutation.

Core Idea
Some groups say God is everywhere by knowledge, not by essence.

This lesson marks the beginning of a new section in which Imam Nasafi refutes two sects: the Mu'tazila and the Najjariyya. Both groups affirm that God is omnipresent, but they specify that this is 'in the sense of knowledge' (بمعنى العلم), not by His essence. Before we examine Nasafi's arguments, we must first clearly identify the view he intends to rebut. This short passage serves as the target statement for the entire refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and its translation. Notice that it is a heading stating the view to be refuted. Identify the two groups mentioned.
The passage is very short but marks a new topic.
2
Identify the claim
The claim is that God Almighty is 'in every place in the sense of knowledge'. Understand what this means: they distinguish between God's essence (not in place) and His knowledge (which encompasses all places).
This is a key nuance that the refutation will address.
3
Understand the context
Recognize that this is the beginning of a refutation. The author is about to dismantle this position. In this lesson, we only set up the view; the arguments come later.
This is a preparatory step for deeper study.
4
Check your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to ensure you remember which sects hold this view. This will help you track the subsequent discussion.

Source Text

الرد على المعتزلة والنجارية وما يقوله المعتزلة، وعامة النجارية أنه تعالى بكل مكان بمعنى العلم
The refutation of the Mu'tazila and the Najjariyya: And what the Mu'tazila and most of the Najjariyya say, that God Almighty is in every place in the sense of knowledge.
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage introduces the next topic: refuting the Mu'tazila and Najjariyya. These groups claim that God is everywhere (بكل مكان) not by His essence, but in the sense of knowledge (بمعنى العلم). That is, they affirm God's omnipresence epistemically rather than physically. The author is about to argue against this position, showing that either it collapses into the same problems as the essence view or leads to other absurdities. This sentence serves as a section heading and statement of the view to be refuted.

Correction: The passage clarifies they say God is everywhere only in the sense of knowledge (بمعنى العلم), not by His essence. They avoid literal spatial omnipresence.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If God truly knows every place and every secret, are there corners of your life you try to hide from Him? What would change if you fully lived as if His knowledge is a personal, loving—but also demanding—presence?
?
The Mu'tazila denied God's essential presence to protect His transcendence. But in doing so, did they lose something of His intimacy? Where in your life have you prioritized intellectual comfort or theological safety over a raw, personal encounter with the living God?

Refutation of the Expression 'God is Everywhere'
Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazila/Najjariyya claim about God being everywhere by knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazilite and Najjariyya expression 'God is everywhere' is problematic even when reinterpreted as knowledge, and why it is obligatory to avoid such wording.

Core Idea
A dangerous expression is not saved by a good intention.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi confronts a subtle mistake made by the Mu'tazila and Najjariyya: they agree with Ahl al-Sunna on the meaning (God is not physically in places) but insist on using a phrase that implies the opposite. Nasafi argues that this is not a mere verbal dispute; the expression itself is harmful and unsupported by revelation. Let's see how he builds his case.

Guided Path
1
Understand the sectarian claim
The Mu'tazila and Najjariyya say that God is in every place in the sense of knowledge, power, and management, not in essence.
Note that they do not claim bodily presence, but a metaphorical 'being'.
2
Notice the agreement in meaning
Both the author and these sects agree that God cannot be physically located in places and that He knows and manages all places.
The disagreement is not over the intended meaning, but over the wording.
3
Grasp the objection
The author objects that the expression 'in every place' is ugly and its outward meaning implies disbelief, and there is no scriptural support for it.
Thus, it is obligatory to avoid such an expression to protect the correct belief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرد على المعتزلة والنجارية
وما يقوله المعتزلة، وعامة النجارية أنه تعالى بكل مكان بمعنى العلم والقدرة، والتدبير دون الذات فهذا منهم خلاف في العبارة، فأما في المعنى فقد ساعدونا على استحالة تمكنه في الأمكنة، ونحن ساعدناهم أنه عالم بالأمكنة كلها وكلها تدبيره غير أن لهم غنية عن إطلاق هذه العبارة الوحشية في هذا المراد. ومن الذي اضطرهم إلى إطلاق هذه العبارة التي ظاهرها يوجب ما هو كفر وضلال، وأي ضرورة دعتهم إلى ذلك، ولم يرد به نص لا في كتاب، ولا في الأحاديث المشهورة؟ فإذا الواجب علينا عند انعدام النص صيانة هذا المعنى عن هذا اللفظ الوحشي، وبالله النجاة والمعونة.
The refutation of the Mu'tazila and the Najjariyya. And what the Mu'tazila and the majority of the Najjariyya say—that He, exalted, is in every place in the sense of knowledge, power, and management, not in essence—this from them is a disagreement in expression. As for the meaning, they have assisted us [in affirming] the impossibility of His being established in places, and we have assisted them that He is knower of all places and that all of them are under His management, except that they have no need to utter this ugly expression for this intended meaning. Who compelled them to utter this expression, whose outward appearance necessitates that which is disbelief and misguidance? What necessity called them to that, when no text has come regarding it, neither in the Book nor in well-known hadiths? Therefore, it is obligatory upon us, when the text is absent, to protect this meaning from this ugly wording. And with Allah is deliverance and assistance.
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila and Najjariyya who say that God is in every place, but they mean by knowledge, power, and management, not by essence. The author notes that there is agreement on the meaning: both sides accept that God cannot physically dwell in places, and that He knows and manages all places. The problem is their choice of wording. They have no need to use such an ugly expression whose apparent sense implies disbelief and misguidance. No scriptural evidence supports this phrasing. Therefore, in the absence of a revealed text, it is obligatory to protect the correct meaning by avoiding such a dangerous expression.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
النجارية
بكل مكان
العلم
القدرة
التدبير
العبارة الوحشية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Mu'tazilite phrase 'God is everywhere' is harmless if reinterpreted as knowledge.
Correction: The expression itself is still problematic because its apparent meaning implies kufr, and there is no scriptural support for it, so it must be avoided.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main objection to the Mu'tazila and Najjariyya?
A) They deny God's knowledge of places.
B) They use a misleading expression that can imply God is physically present in places.
C) They claim God is in places by essence.
D) They reject the possibility of God's management.

Key Takeaway
It is obligatory to avoid expressions about Allah that imply falsehood, even if the intended meaning is correct, especially when there is no textual justification for them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of the phrases you use when talking about Allah—like 'He is in my heart' or 'He is with me everywhere.' Are these expressions found in the Qur'an and Sunnah? Will you abandon any that, upon reflection, imply a limitation or location for Allah, even if your intention was purely devotional?
?
You now know that the Mu'tazila had a correct understanding (God's knowledge and power encompass all) but used a wrong expression. Are there areas in your own religious practice where you prioritize emotional comfort over precise, revealed wording? Are you willing to correct that starting today?
?
The passage says 'no text has come regarding it'—meaning the expression has no basis. Do you ever adopt common religious sayings or phrases from your culture without verifying their source? What will you do the next time you hear such a phrase being used about Allah?
